                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2012 485:7400"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2012-05-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2012 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="This paper reports the genome sequence of domesticated tomato, a major crop plant, and a draft sequence for its closest wild relative; comparative genomics reveal very little divergence between the two genomes but some important differences with the potato genome, another important food crop in the genus Solanum. Tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) is a major crop plant and a model system for fruit development. Solanum is one of the largest angiosperm genera1 and includes annual and perennial plants from diverse habitats. Here we present a high-quality genome sequence of domesticated tomato, a draft sequence of its closest wild relative, Solanum pimpinellifolium2, and compare them to each other and to the potato genome (Solanum tuberosum). The two tomato genomes show only 0.6% nucleotide divergence and signs of recent admixture, but show more than 8% divergence from potato, with nine large and several smaller inversions. In contrast to Arabidopsis, but similar to soybean, tomato and potato small RNAs map predominantly to gene-rich chromosomal regions, including gene promoters. The Solanum lineage has experienced two consecutive genome triplications: one that is ancient and shared with rosids, and a more recent one. These triplications set the stage for the neofunctionalization of genes controlling fruit characteristics, such as colour and fleshiness."/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution</h1>
                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-author-popup="group-1" href="#group-1">The Tomato Genome Consortium</a></li></ul>

    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/nature"><i data-test="journal-title">Nature</i></a>

                        <b data-test="journal-volume"><span class="u-visually-hidden">volume</span> 485</b>, <span class="u-visually-hidden">pages</span><span itemprop="pageStart">635</span>–<span itemprop="pageEnd">641</span>(<span data-test="article-publication-year">2012</span>)<a href="#citeas" class="c-article-info-details__cite-as u-hide-print" data-track="click" data-track-action="cite this article" data-track-label="link">Cite this article</a>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>) is a major crop plant and a model system for fruit development. <i>Solanum</i> is one of the largest angiosperm genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Frodin, D. G. History and concepts of big plant genera. Taxon 53, 753–776 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6470">1</a></sup> and includes annual and perennial plants from diverse habitats. Here we present a high-quality genome sequence of domesticated tomato, a draft sequence of its closest wild relative, <i>Solanum</i> <i>pimpinellifolium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Peralta, I. E., Spooner, D. M. &amp; Knapp, S. Taxonomy of tomatoes: a revision of wild tomatoes (Solanum section Lycopersicon) and their outgroup relatives in sections Juglandifolia and Lycopersicoides . Syst. Bot. Monogr. 84, 1–186 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6479">2</a></sup>, and compare them to each other and to the potato genome (<i>Solanum</i> <i>tuberosum</i>). The two tomato genomes show only 0.6% nucleotide divergence and signs of recent admixture, but show more than 8% divergence from potato, with nine large and several smaller inversions. In contrast to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, but similar to soybean, tomato and potato small RNAs map predominantly to gene-rich chromosomal regions, including gene promoters. The <i>Solanum</i> lineage has experienced two consecutive genome triplications: one that is ancient and shared with rosids, and a more recent one. These triplications set the stage for the neofunctionalization of genes controlling fruit characteristics, such as colour and fleshiness.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/nature11119.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genome of the inbred tomato cultivar ‘Heinz 1706’ was sequenced and assembled using a combination of Sanger and ‘next generation’ technologies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 1</a>). The predicted genome size is approximately 900 megabases (Mb), consistent with previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Michaelson, M. J., Price, H. J., Ellison, J. R. &amp; Johnston, J. S. Comparison of plant DNA contents determined by Feulgen microspectrophotometry and laser flow cytometry. Am. J. Bot. 78, 183–188 (1991)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6525">3</a></sup>, of which 760 Mb were assembled in 91 scaffolds aligned to the 12 tomato chromosomes, with most gaps restricted to pericentromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Base accuracy is approximately one substitution error per 29.4 kilobases (kb) and one indel error per 6.4 kb. The scaffolds were linked with two bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-based physical maps and anchored/oriented using a high-density genetic map, introgression line mapping and BAC fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="
                        Tomato genome topography and synteny.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>Tomato genome topography and synteny.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="801"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>A</b>, Multi-dimensional topography of tomato chromosome 1 (chromosomes 2–12 are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). <b>a</b>, Left: contrast-reversed, 4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI)-stained pachytene chromosome; centre and right: FISH signals for repeat sequences on diagrammatic pachytene chromosomes (purple, TGR1; blue, TGR4; red, telomere repeat; green, Cot 100 DNA (including most repeats)). <b>b</b>, Frequency distribution of recombination nodules (RNs) representing crossovers on 249 chromosomes. Red stars mark 5 cM intervals starting from the end of the short arm (top). Scale is in micrometres. <b>c</b>, FISH-based locations of selected BACs (horizontal blue lines on left). <b>d</b>, Kazusa F2-2000 linkage map. Blue lines to the left connect linkage map markers on the BAC-FISH map (<b>c</b>), and to the right to heat maps (<b>e</b>) and the DNA pseudomolecule (<b>f</b>). <b>e</b>, From left to right: linkage map distance (cM/Mb, turquoise), repeated sequences (% nucleotides per 500 kb, purple), genes (% nucleotides per 500 kb, blue), chloroplast insertions; RNA-Seq reads from leaves and breaker fruits of <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> (number of reads per 500 kb, green and red, respectively), microRNA genes (transcripts per million per 500 kb, black), small RNAs (thin horizontal black and red lines, sum of hits-normalized abundances). Horizontal grey lines represent gaps in the pseudomolecule (<b>f</b>). <b>f</b>, DNA pseudomolecule consisting of nine scaffolds. Unsequenced gaps (approximately 9.8 Mb, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 13</a>) are indicated by white horizontal lines. Tomato genes identified by map-based cloning (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 14</a>) are indicated on the right. For more details, see legend to <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>. <b>B</b>, Syntenic relationships in the <i>Solanaceae</i>. COSII-based comparative maps of potato, aubergine (eggplant), pepper and <i>Nicotiana</i> with respect to the tomato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 4.5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Each tomato chromosome is assigned a different colour and orthologous chromosome segment(s) in other species are shown in the same colour. White dots indicate approximate centromere locations. Each black arrow indicates an inversion relative to tomato and ‘+1’ indicates a minimum of one inversion. Each black bar beside a chromosome indicates translocation breakpoints relative to tomato. Chromosome lengths are not to scale, but segments within chromosomes are. <b>C</b>, Tomato–potato syntenic relationships dot plot of tomato (T) and potato (P) genomic sequences based on collinear blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 4.1</a>). Red and blue dots represent gene pairs with statistically significant high and low <i>ω</i> (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub><i>/K</i><sub>s</sub>) in collinear blocks, which average <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ≤ 0.5, respectively. Green and magenta dots represent genes in collinear blocks which average 0.5 &lt; <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ≤ 1.5 and <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> &gt; 1.5, respectively. Yellow dots represent all other gene pairs. Blocks circled in red are examples of pan-eudicot triplication. Inserts represent schematic drawings of BAC-FISH patterns of cytologically demonstrated chromosome inversions (also in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM63">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The genome of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> LA1589 was sequenced and assembled <i>de novo</i> using Illumina short reads, yielding a 739 Mb draft genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 3</a>). Estimated divergence between the wild and domesticated genomes is 0.6% (5.4 million single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) distributed along the chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>)).</p><p>Tomato chromosomes consist of pericentric heterochromatin and distal euchromatin, with repeats concentrated within and around centromeres, in chromomeres and at telomeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Substantially higher densities of recombination, genes and transcripts are observed in euchromatin, whereas chloroplast insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information sections 1.22 and 1.23</a>) and conserved microRNA (miRNA) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.9</a>) are more evenly distributed throughout the genome. The genome is highly syntenic with those of other economically important Solanaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1B</a>). Compared to the genomes of <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana . Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6709">4</a></sup> and <i>Sorghum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6715">5</a></sup>, tomato has fewer high-copy, full-length long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons with older average insertion ages (2.8 versus 0.8 million years (Myr) ago) and fewer high-frequency <i>k</i>-mers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.10</a>). This supports previous findings that the tomato genome is unusual among angiosperms by being largely comprised of low-copy DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zamir, D. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Tomato genome is comprised largely of fast-evolving, low copy-number sequences. Mol. Gen. Genet. 213, 254–261 (1988)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6725">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Peterson, D. G., Pearson, W. R. &amp; Stack, S. M. Characterization of the tomato (Lycopersicon esculentum) genome using in vitro and in situ DNA reassociation. Genome 41, 346–356 (1998)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6728">7</a></sup>.</p><p>The pipeline used to annotate the tomato and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6735">8</a></sup> genomes is described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2</a>. It predicted 34,727 and 35,004 protein-coding genes, respectively. Of these, 30,855 and 32,988, respectively, are supported by RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) data, and 31,741 and 32,056, respectively, show high similarity to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.1</a>). Chromosomal organization of genes, transcripts, repeats and small RNAs (sRNAs) is very similar in the two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Figs 2–4</a>). The protein-coding genes of tomato, potato, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and grape were clustered into 23,208 gene groups (≥2 members), of which 8,615 are common to all five genomes, 1,727 are confined to eudicots (tomato, potato, grape and <i>Arabidopsis</i>), and 727 are confined to plants with fleshy fruits (tomato, potato and grape) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 5.1 and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Relative expression of all tomato genes was determined by replicated strand-specific Illumina RNA-Seq of root, leaf, flower (two stages) and fruit (six stages) in addition to leaf and fruit (three stages) of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><p>sRNA sequencing data supported the prediction of 96 conserved miRNA genes in tomato and 120 in potato, a number consistent with other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Figs 1 and 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.9</a>). Among the 34 miRNA families identified, 10 are highly conserved in plants and similarly represented in the two species, whereas other, less conserved families are more abundant in potato. Several miRNAs, predicted to target Toll interleukin receptor, nucleotide-binding site and leucine-rich repeat (TIR-NBS-LRR) genes, seemed to be preferentially or exclusively expressed in potato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.9</a>).</p><p>Comparative genomic studies are reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 4</a>. Sequence alignment of 71 Mb of euchromatic tomato genomic DNA to their potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6792">8</a></sup> counterparts revealed 8.7% nucleotide divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 4.1</a>). Intergenic and repeat-rich heterochromatic sequences showed more than 30% nucleotide divergence, consistent with the high sequence diversity in these regions among potato genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6799">8</a></sup>. Alignment of tomato–potato orthologous regions confirmed nine large inversions known from cytological or genetic studies and several smaller ones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1C</a>). The exact number of small inversions is difficult to determine due to the lack of orientation of most potato scaffolds.</p><p>A total of 18,320 clearly orthologous tomato–potato gene pairs were identified. Of these, 138 (0.75%) had significantly higher than average non-synonymous (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>) versus synonymous (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) nucleotide substitution rate ratios (<i>ω</i>), indicating diversifying selection, whereas 147 (0.80%) had significantly lower than average <i>ω</i>, indicating purifying selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The proportions of high and low <i>ω</i> between sorghum and maize (<i>Zea mays</i>) are 0.70% and 1.19%, respectively, after 11.9 Myr of divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Swigoňová, Z. et al. Close split of sorghum and maize genome progenitors. Genome Res. 14, 1916–1923 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6833">9</a></sup>, indicating that diversifying selection may have been stronger in tomato–potato. The highest densities of low-<i>ω</i> genes are found in collinear blocks with average <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> &gt; 1.5, tracing to a genome triplication shared with grape (see below) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig1">Fig. 1C</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 3</a>). These genes, which have been preserved in paleo-duplicated locations for more than 100 Myr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6854">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6857">11</a></sup>, are more constrained than ‘average’ genes and are enriched for transcription factors and genes otherwise related to gene regulation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Tables 3 and 4</a>).</p><p>Sequence comparison of 31,760 Heinz 1706 genes with &gt;5× <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> read coverage in over 90% of their coding regions revealed 7,378 identical genes and 11,753 with only synonymous changes. The remaining 12,629 genes had non-synonymous changes, including gains and losses of stop codons with potential consequences for gene function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Tables 5–7</a>). Several pericentric regions, predicted to contain genes, are absent or polymorphic in the broader <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> germplasm (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Within cultivated germplasm, particularly among the small-fruited cherry tomatoes, several chromosomal segments are more closely related to <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> than to Heinz 1706 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Figs 8 and 9</a>), supporting previous observations on recent admixture of these gene pools due to breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ranc, N., Munos, S., Santoni, S. &amp; Causse, M. A clarified position for Solanum lycopersicum var. cerasiforme in the evolutionary history of tomatoes (solanaceae). BMC Plant Biol. 8, 130 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6889">12</a></sup>. Heinz 1706 itself has been reported to carry introgressions from <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ozminkowski, R. Pedigree of variety Heinz 1706. Rep. Tomato Genet. Coop. 54, 26 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6895">13</a></sup>, traces of which are detectable on chromosomes 4, 9, 11 and 12 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>Comparison of the tomato and grape genomes supports the hypothesis that a whole-genome triplication affecting the rosid lineage occurred in a common eudicot ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6905">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The distribution of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> between corresponding gene pairs in duplicated blocks suggests that one polyploidization in the solanaceous lineage preceded the rosid–asterid (tomato–grape) divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="
                        The 
                        Solanum
                         whole genome triplication.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>The </b>
                        <b><i>Solanum</i></b>
                        <b> whole genome triplication.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="334"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Speciation and polyploidization in eudicot lineages. Confirmed whole-genome duplications and triplications are shown with annotated circles, including ‘<i>T</i>’ (this paper) and previously discovered events <i>α</i>, <i>β</i>, <i>γ</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6954">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6957">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Moore, M. J., Soltis, P. S., Bell, C. D., Burleigh, J. G. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Phylogenetic analysis of 83 plastid genes further resolves the early diversification of eudicots. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 4623–4628 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6960">14</a></sup>. Dashed circles represent one or more suspected polyploidies reported in previous publications that need further support from genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6965">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Aagaard, J. E., Willis, J. H. &amp; Phillips, P. C. Relaxed selection among duplicate floral regulatory genes in Lamiales. J. Mol. Evol. 63, 493–503 (2006)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6968">28</a></sup>. Grey branches indicate unpublished genomes. Black and red error bars bracket indicate the likely timings of divergence of major asterid lineages and of ‘<i>T</i>’, respectively. The post-‘<i>T</i>’ subgenomes, designated T1, T2, and T3, are further detailed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>. <b>b</b>, On the basis of alignments of multiple tomato genome segments to single grape genome segments, the tomato genome is partitioned into three non-overlapping ‘subgenomes’ (T1, T2, T3), each represented by one axis in the three-dimensional plot. The ancestral gene order of each subgenome is inferred according to orthologous grape regions, with tomato chromosomal affinities shown by red (inner) bars. Segments tracing to pan-eudicot triplication (<i>γ</i>) are shown by green (outer) bars with colours representing the seven putative pre-<i>γ</i> eudicot ancestral chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2562e6991">10</a></sup>, also coded <i>a</i>–<i>g</i>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM64">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparison with the grape genome also reveals a more recent triplication in tomato and potato. Whereas few individual tomato/potato genes remain triplicated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Tables 10 and 11</a>), 73% of tomato gene models are in blocks that are orthologous to one grape region, collectively covering 84% of the grape gene space. Among these grape genomic regions, 22.5% have one orthologous region in tomato, 39.9% have two, and 21.6% have three, indicating that a whole-genome triplication occurred in the <i>Solanum</i> lineage, followed by widespread gene loss. This triplication, also evident in potato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), is estimated at 71 (±19.4) Myr on the basis of the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> of paralogous genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), and therefore predates the <span class="stix">∼</span>7.3 Myr tomato–potato divergence. On the basis of alignments to single grape genome segments, the tomato genome can be partitioned into three non-overlapping ‘subgenomes’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The number of euasterid lineages that have experienced the recent triplication remains unclear and awaits complete euasterid I and II genome sequences. <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions show that euasterids I and II, and indeed the rosid–asterid lineages, all diverged from common ancestry at or near the pan-eudicot triplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>), suggesting that this event may have contributed to the formation of major eudicot lineages in a short period of several million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Moore, M. J., Soltis, P. S., Bell, C. D., Burleigh, J. G. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Phylogenetic analysis of 83 plastid genes further resolves the early diversification of eudicots. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 4623–4628 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7045">14</a></sup>, partially explaining the explosive radiation of angiosperm plants on Earth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Stockey, R. A., Graham, S. W. &amp; Crane, P. R. Introduction to the Darwin special issue: the abominable mystery. Am. J. Bot. 96, 3–4 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7049">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Fleshy fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>) are an important means of attracting vertebrate frugivores for seed dispersal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Howe, H. F. &amp; Smallwood, J. Ecology of seed dispersal. Annu. Rev. Ecol. Syst. 13, 201–228 (1982)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7060">16</a></sup>. Combined orthology and synteny analyses indicate that both genome triplications added new gene family members that mediate important fruit-specific functions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). These include transcription factors and enzymes necessary for ethylene biosynthesis (<i>RIN</i>, <i>CNR</i>, <i>ACS</i>) and perception (<i>ETR3/NR</i>, <i>ETR4</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Klee, H. J. &amp; Giovannoni, J. J. Genetics and control of tomato fruit ripening and quality attributes. Annu. Rev. Genet. 45, 41–59 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7083">17</a></sup>, red light photoreceptors influencing fruit quality (<i>PHYB1/PHYB2</i>) and ethylene- and light-regulated genes mediating lycopene biosynthesis (<i>PSY1/PSY2</i>). Several cytochrome P450 subfamilies associated with toxic alkaloid biosynthesis show contraction or complete loss in tomato and the extant genes show negligible expression in ripe fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 5.4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="
                        Whole-genome triplications set the stage for fruit-specific gene neofunctionalization.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>Whole-genome triplications set the stage for fruit-specific gene neofunctionalization.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1052"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The genes shown represent a fruit ripening control network regulated by transcription factors (<i>MADS-RIN</i>, <i>CNR</i>) necessary for production of the ripening hormone ethylene, the production of which is regulated by ACC synthase (<i>ACS</i>). Ethylene interacts with ethylene receptors (<i>ETR</i>s) to drive expression changes in output genes, including phytoene synthase (<i>PSY</i>), the rate-limiting step in carotenoid biosynthesis. Light, acting through phytochromes, controls fruit pigmentation through an ethylene-independent pathway. Paralogous gene pairs with different physiological roles (<i>MADS1/RIN</i>, <i>PHYB1/PHYB2</i>, <i>ACS2/ACS6</i>, <i>ETR3/ETR4</i>, <i>PSY1/PSY2</i>), were generated during the eudicot (<i>γ</i>, black circle) or the more recent <i>Solanum</i> (<i>T</i>, red circle) triplications. Complete dendrograms of the respective protein families are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Figs 16 and 17</a>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM65">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Fruit texture has profound agronomic and sensory importance and is controlled in part by cell wall structure and composition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vicente, A. R., Saladie, M., Rose, J. K. C. &amp; Labavitch, J. M. The linkage between cell wall metabolism and fruit softening: looking to the future. J. Sci. Food Agric. 87, 1435–1448 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7174">18</a></sup>. More than 50 genes showing differential expression during fruit development and ripening encode proteins involved in modification of cell wall architecture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 5.7</a>). For example, a family of xyloglucan endotransglucosylase/hydrolases (XTHs) has expanded both in the recent whole-genome triplication and through tandem duplication. One of the triplicated members, <i>XTH10</i>, shows differential loss between tomato and potato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 12</a>), suggesting genetically driven specialization in the remodelling of fruit cell walls.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="
                        The tomato genome allows systems approaches to fruit biology.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: 
                        <b>The tomato genome allows systems approaches to fruit biology.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11119/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11119_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="363"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Xyloglucan transglucosylase/hydrolases (XTHs) differentially expressed between mature green and ripe fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 5.7</a>). These <i>XTH</i> genes and many others are expressed in ripening fruits and are linked with the <i>Solanum</i> triplication, marked with a red circle on the phylogenetic tree. Red lines on the tree denote paralogues derived from the <i>Solanum</i> triplication, and blue lines are tandem duplications. <b>b</b>, Developmentally regulated accumulation of sRNAs mapping to the promoter region of a fruit-regulated cell wall gene (pectin acetylesterase, Solyc08g005800). Variation of abundance of sRNAs (left) and messenger RNA expression levels from the corresponding gene (right) over a tomato fruit developmental series (T1, bud; T2, flower; T3, fruit 1–3 mm; T4, fruit 5–7 mm; T5, fruit 11–13 mm; T6, fruit mature green; T7, breaker; T8, breaker + 3 days; T9, breaker + 7 days). The promoter regions are grouped in 100-nucleotide windows. For each window the size class distribution of sRNAs is shown (red, 21; green, 22; orange, 23; blue, 24). The height of the box corresponding to the first time point shows the cumulative sRNA abundance in log scale. The height of the following boxes is proportional to the log offset fold change (offset = 20) relative to the first time point. The expression profile of the mRNA is shown in log<sub>2</sub> scale. The horizontal black line represents 1 kb of the promoter region. 0 to 12 represent arbitrary units of gene expression.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM66">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11119/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Similar to soybean and potato and in contrast to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, tomato sRNAs map preferentially to euchromatin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). sRNAs from tomato flowers and fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mohorianu, I. et al. Profiling of short RNAs during fleshy fruit development reveals stage-specific sRNAome expression patterns. Plant J. 67, 232–246 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7253">19</a></sup> map to 8,416 gene promoters. Differential expression of sRNAs during fruit development is apparent for 2,687 promoters, including those of cell-wall-related genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>) and occurs preferentially at key developmental transitions (for example, flower to fruit, fruit growth to fruit ripening, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.8</a>).</p><p>The genome sequences of tomato, <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> and potato provide a starting point for comparing gene family evolution and sub-functionalization in the <i>Solanaceae</i>. A striking example is the <i>SELF PRUNING</i> (<i>SP</i>) gene family, which includes the homologue of <i>Arabidopsis FT</i>, encoding the mobile flowering signal florigen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Corbesier, L. et al. FT protein movement contributes to long-distance signaling in floral induction of Arabidopsis . Science 316, 1030–1033 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7282">20</a></sup> and its antagonist <i>SP</i>, encoding the orthologue of <i>TFL1</i>. Nearly a century ago, a spontaneous mutation in <i>SP</i> spawned the ‘determinate’ varieties that now dominate the tomato mechanical harvesting industry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rick, C. M. The tomato. Sci. Am. 239, 76–87 (1978)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7295">21</a></sup>. The genome sequence has revealed that the <i>SP</i> family has expanded in the <i>Solanum</i> lineage compared to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, driven by the <i>Solanum</i> triplication and tandem duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). In potato, <i>SP3D</i> and <i>SP6A</i> control flowering and tuberization, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Navarro, C. et al. Control of flowering and storage organ formation in potato by FLOWERING LOCUS T. Nature 478, 119–122 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7322">22</a></sup>, whereas <i>SP3D</i> in tomato, known as <i>SINGLE FLOWER TRUSS</i>, similarly controls flowering, but also drives heterosis for fruit yield in an epistatic relationship with <i>SP</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lifschitz, E. et al. The tomato FT ortholog triggers systemic signals that regulate growth and flowering and substitute for diverse environmental stimuli. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 6398–6403 (2006)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7334">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Krieger, U., Lippman, Z. B. &amp; Zamir, D. The flowering gene SINGLE FLOWER TRUSS drives heterosis for yield in tomato. Nature Genet. 42, 459–463 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7337">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Pnueli, L. et al. The SELF-PRUNING gene of tomato regulates vegetative to reproductive switching of sympodial meristems and is the ortholog of CEN and TFL1 . Development 125, 1979–1989 (1998)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7340">25</a></sup>. Interestingly, <i>SP6A</i> in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> is inactivated by a premature stop codon, but remains functionally intact in <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i>. Thus, allelic variation in a subset of <i>SP</i> family genes has played a major role in the generation of both shared and species-specific variation in solanaceous agricultural traits.</p><p>The genome sequences of tomato and <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> also provide a basis for understanding the bottlenecks that have narrowed tomato genetic diversity: the domestication of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> in the Americas, the export of a small number of genotypes to Europe in the 16th century, and the intensive breeding that followed. Charles Rick pioneered the use of trait introgression from wild tomato relatives to increase genetic diversity of cultivated tomatoes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Rick, C. M. Hybridization between Lycopersicon esculentum and Solanum pennellii: phylogenetic and cytogenetic significance. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 46, 78–82 (1960)" href="/articles/nature11119#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2562e7366">26</a></sup>. Introgression lines exist for seven wild tomato species, including <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i>, in the background of cultivated tomato. The genome sequences presented here and the availability of millions of SNPs will allow breeders to revisit this rich trait reservoir and identify domestication genes, providing biological knowledge and empowering biodiversity-based breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>A total of 21 gigabases (Gb) of Roche/454 Titanium shotgun and mate pair reads and 3.3 Gb of Sanger paired-end reads, including <span class="stix">∼</span>200,000 BAC and fosmid end sequence pairs, were generated from the ‘Heinz 1706’ inbred line (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information sections 1.1–1.7</a>), assembled using both Newbler and CABOG and integrated into a single assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information sections 1.17 and 1.18</a>). The scaffolds were anchored using two BAC-based physical maps, one high density genetic map, overgo hybridization and genome-wide BAC FISH (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> sections 1.8–1.16 and 1.19). Over 99.9% of BAC/fosmid end pairs mapped consistently on the assembly and over 98% of EST sequences could be aligned to the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 1.20</a>). Chloroplast genome insertions in the nuclear genome were validated using a mate pair method and the flanking regions were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information sections 1.22–1.24</a>). Annotation was carried out using a pipeline based on EuGene that integrates <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, RNA-Seq alignment and rich function annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2</a>). To facilitate interspecies comparison, the potato genome was re-annotated using the same pipeline. LTR retrotransposons were detected <i>de novo</i> with the LTR-STRUC program and dated by the sequence divergence between left and right solo LTR (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 2.10</a>). The genome of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> was sequenced to ×40 depth using Illumina paired end reads and assembled using ABySS (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 3</a>). The tomato and potato genomes were aligned using LASTZ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 4.1</a>). Identification of triplicated regions was done using BLASTP, in-house-generated scripts and three-way comparisons between tomato, potato and <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> using MCSCAN (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information sections 4.2–4.4</a>). Specific gene families/groups (genes for ascorbate, carotenoid and jasmonate biosynthesis, cytochrome P450s, genes controlling cell wall architecture, hormonal and transcriptional regulators, resistance genes) were subjected to expert curation/analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 5</a>). PHYML and MEGA were used to reconstruct phylogenetic trees and MCSCAN was used to infer gene collinearity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11119#MOESM67">Supplementary Information section 5.2</a>).</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>The genomic data generated by the whole project are available in GenBank as accession number AEKE00000000, and the individual chromosome sequences as numbersCM001064–CM001075. TheRNA-Seq data are available in the Sequence Read Archive under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=SRA049915">SRA049915</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GSE33507">GSE33507</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=SRA050797">SRA050797</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=SRA048144">SRA048144</a>.                    data-track-label="link:Genome-wide identification of genes involved in heterotrimeric G-protein signaling in Tartary buckwheat (Fagopyrum tataricum) and their potential roles in regulating fruit development"
                   href="https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijbiomac.2021.01.016"
                   id="title-link-4">
                  Genome-wide identification of genes involved in heterotrimeric G-protein signaling in Tartary buckwheat (Fagopyrum tataricum) and their potential roles in regulating fruit development
                </a>
              </h3>
